</ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_McDonald%27s_products" title="List of McDonald's products">List of McDonald's products</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HK_Kln_Bay_Telford_Plaza_McDonalds_Restaurant_McSPICY_Chicken_Filet_French_fries_Nov-2014_Love_mark_sign_n_coke.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/HK_Kln_Bay_Telford_Plaza_McDonalds_Restaurant_McSPICY_Chicken_Filet_French_fries_Nov-2014_Love_mark_sign_n_coke.jpg/220px-HK_Kln_Bay_Telford_Plaza_McDonalds_Restaurant_McSPICY_Chicken_Filet_French_fries_Nov-2014_Love_mark_sign_n_coke.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="1836"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/HK_Kln_Bay_Telford_Plaza_McDonalds_Restaurant_McSPICY_Chicken_Filet_French_fries_Nov-2014_Love_mark_sign_n_coke.jpg/220px-HK_Kln_Bay_Telford_Plaza_McDonalds_Restaurant_McSPICY_Chicken_Filet_French_fries_Nov-2014_Love_mark_sign_n_coke.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HK_Kln_Bay_Telford_Plaza_McDonalds_Restaurant_McSPICY_Chicken_Filet_French_fries_Nov-2014_Love_mark_sign_n_coke.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical "eat-in" McDonald's meal as sold in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, consisting of <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">French fries</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drink</a>, and a "main product" – in this case, a McSpicy Chicken Fillet. Condiments are supplied in small packets; such a packet of <a href="/wiki/Tomato_ketchup" class="mw-redirect" title="Tomato ketchup">tomato ketchup</a> is seen in the foreground.</div></div></div>
<p>McDonald's predominantly sells <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburgers</a>, various types of <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicken_sandwich" title="Chicken sandwich">chicken sandwiches</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">French fries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breakfast" title="Breakfast">breakfast</a> items, and <a href="/wiki/Dessert" title="Dessert">desserts</a>. In most markets, McDonald's offers <a href="/wiki/Salad" title="Salad">salads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vegetarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Vegetarian">vegetarian</a> items, <a href="/wiki/Wrap_(food)" title="Wrap (food)">wraps</a> and other localized fare. On a seasonal basis, McDonald's offers the <a href="/wiki/McRib" title="McRib">McRib</a> sandwich. Some speculate the seasonality of the McRib adds to its appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p><p>Products are offered as either "dine-in" (where the customer opts to eat in the restaurant) or "take-out" (where the customer opts to take the food off the premises). "Dine-in" meals are provided on a plastic tray with a paper insert on the floor of the tray. "Take-out" meals are usually delivered with the contents enclosed in a distinctive McDonald's-branded brown paper bag. In both cases, the individual items are wrapped or boxed as appropriate.
</p><p>Since <a href="/wiki/Steve_Easterbrook" title="Steve Easterbrook">Steve Easterbrook</a> became CEO of the company, McDonald's has streamlined the menu which in the United States contained nearly 200 items. The company has looked to introduce healthier options, and removed <a href="/wiki/High-fructose_corn_syrup" title="High-fructose corn syrup">high-fructose corn syrup</a> from <a href="/wiki/Bun" title="Bun">hamburger buns</a>. The company has removed <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">artificial preservatives</a> from <a href="/wiki/Chicken_McNuggets" title="Chicken McNuggets">Chicken McNuggets</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> replacing chicken skin, safflower oil and citric acid found in Chicken McNuggets with pea starch, rice starch and powdered lemon juice.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2018, McDonald's USA announced that they no longer use artificial preservatives, flavors and colors entirely from seven classic burgers sold in the U.S., including the hamburger, cheeseburger, double cheeseburger, McDouble, Quarter Pounder with Cheese, double Quarter Pounder with Cheese and the Big Mac.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the pickles will still be made with an artificial preservative, although customers can choose to opt out of getting pickles with their burgers.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="International_menu_variations">International menu variations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_products_(international)" class="mw-redirect" title="McDonald's products (international)">McDonald's products (international)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebi_burger.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ebi_burger.jpg/220px-Ebi_burger.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3920" data-file-height="2204"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Ebi_burger.jpg/220px-Ebi_burger.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebi_burger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A McDonald's <a href="/wiki/Ebi_korokke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebi korokke">Ebi</a> Feast meal sold at branches in Singapore, November 2013. McDonald's is known for tailoring its menus in different markets to cater to local tastes</div></div></div>
<p>Restaurants in several countries, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, serve soup. This local deviation from the standard menu is a characteristic for which the chain is particularly known, and one which is employed either to abide by regional food taboos (such as the <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism_and_religion" title="Vegetarianism and religion">religious prohibition of beef consumption</a> in India) or to make available foods with which the regional market is more familiar (such as the sale of McRice in Indonesia, or <a href="/wiki/Ebi_korokke" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebi korokke">Ebi</a> (prawn) Burger in Singapore and Japan).
</p><p>In Germany and some other Western European countries, McDonald's sells beer. In New Zealand, McDonald's sells <a href="/wiki/Meat_pie_(Australia_and_New_Zealand)" title="Meat pie (Australia and New Zealand)">meat pies</a>, after local affiliate <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_New_Zealand" title="McDonald's New Zealand">McDonald's New Zealand</a> partially relaunched the <a href="/wiki/Georgie_Pie" title="Georgie Pie">Georgie Pie</a> fast food chain it bought out in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p><p>In the United States and Canada, after limited trials on a regional basis, McDonald's began offering in 2015<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> respectively, a partial breakfast menu during all hours its restaurants are open.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurants">Restaurants</span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<table class="box-Globalize plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this section <b>deal primarily with the United States and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=McDonald%27s&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:McDonald%27s" title="Talk:McDonald's">talk page</a>, or create a new section, as appropriate.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2015</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_restaurants">Types of restaurants</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qatar,_Dukhan_(1),_McDonalds.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Qatar%2C_Dukhan_%281%29%2C_McDonalds.JPG/220px-Qatar%2C_Dukhan_%281%29%2C_McDonalds.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1996" data-file-height="1532"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 169px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Qatar%2C_Dukhan_%281%29%2C_McDonalds.JPG/220px-Qatar%2C_Dukhan_%281%29%2C_McDonalds.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="169" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qatar,_Dukhan_(1),_McDonalds.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Counter service in a McDonald's restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Dukhan" title="Dukhan">Dukhan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a></div></div></div>
<p>Most standalone McDonald's restaurants offer both <a href="/wiki/Counter_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Counter service">counter service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drive-through" title="Drive-through">drive-through</a> service, with indoor and sometimes outdoor seating.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-81">[81]</a></sup> Drive-Thru, Auto-Mac, Pay and Drive, or "McDrive" as it is known in many countries, often has separate stations for placing, paying for, and picking up orders, though the latter two steps are frequently combined;<sup id="cite_ref-:0_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-81">[81]</a></sup> it was first introduced in <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Vista,_Arizona" title="Sierra Vista, Arizona">Sierra Vista</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> in 1975,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> following the lead of other fast-food chains. The first such restaurant in Britain opened at <a href="/wiki/Fallowfield" title="Fallowfield">Fallowfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester" title="Manchester">Manchester</a> in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="McDrive">McDrive</span></h4>
<p>In some countries, McDrive locations near highways offer no counter service or seating.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In contrast, locations in high-density city neighborhoods often omit drive-through service.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> There are also a few locations, mostly in downtown districts, that offer a "Walk-Thru" service in place of Drive-Thru.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="McCaf.C3.A9"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="McCafé">McCafé</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/McCaf%C3%A9" title="McCafé">McCafé</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MontevideoMcCafe.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/MontevideoMcCafe.JPG/220px-MontevideoMcCafe.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2932" data-file-height="2197"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/MontevideoMcCafe.JPG/220px-MontevideoMcCafe.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MontevideoMcCafe.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Montevideo" title="Montevideo">Montevideo</a> McCafé</div></div></div>
<p>McCafé is a <a href="/wiki/Coffeehouse" title="Coffeehouse">café</a>-style accompaniment to McDonald's restaurants. The concept was created by McDonald's Australia, where it is marketed as Macca's, starting with <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a> in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> As of 2016, most McDonald's in Australia have McCafés located within the existing McDonald's restaurant. In <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>, there are McCafés in every restaurant, with the rest of the states quickly following suit.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-81">[81]</a></sup> After upgrading to the new McCafé look and feel, some Australian restaurants have noticed up to a 60 percent increase in sales. At the end of 2003, there were over 600 McCafés worldwide.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id=".22Create_Your_Taste.22_restaurants"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Create_Your_Taste"_restaurants'>"Create Your Taste" restaurants</span></h4>
<p>From 2015–2016, McDonald's tried a new gourmet burger service and restaurant concept based on other gourmet restaurants such as <a href="/wiki/Shake_Shack" title="Shake Shack">Shake Shack</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grill%27d" title="Grill'd">Grill'd</a>. It was rolled out for the first time in Australia during the early months of 2015 and expanded to China, Hong Kong, Singapore, Arabia and New Zealand, with ongoing trials in the US market. In dedicated "Create Your Taste" (CYT) kiosks, customers could choose all ingredients including type of bun and meat along with optional extras. In late 2015 the Australian CYT service introduced CYT salads.
</p><p>After a person had ordered, McDonald's advised that wait times were between 10–15 minutes. When the food was ready, trained crew ('hosts') brought the food to the customer's table. Instead of McDonald's usual cardboard and plastic packaging, CYT food was presented on wooden boards, fries in wire baskets and salads in china bowls with metal cutlery. A higher price applied.
</p><p>In November 2016, Create Your Taste was replaced by a "Signature Crafted Recipes" program designed to be more efficient and less expensive.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h4>
<p>Some locations are connected to <a href="/wiki/Gas_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas station">gas stations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Convenience_store" title="Convenience store">convenience stores</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> while others called McExpress have limited seating and/or menu or may be located in a <a href="/wiki/Shopping_mall" title="Shopping mall">shopping mall</a>. Other McDonald's are located in <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">Walmart</a> stores. McStop is a location targeted at truckers and travelers which may have services found at <a href="/wiki/Truck_stop" title="Truck stop">truck stops</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p><p>In Sweden, customers who order a happy meal can use the meal's container for a pair of goggles.<sup id="cite_ref-High_Tech_Capabilities_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-High_Tech_Capabilities-91">[91]</a></sup> The company created a game for the goggles known as <i>Slope Stars</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-High_Tech_Capabilities_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-High_Tech_Capabilities-91">[91]</a></sup> " McDonald's predicts happy goggles will continue in other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-High_Tech_Capabilities_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-High_Tech_Capabilities-91">[91]</a></sup> In the Netherlands, McDonald's has introduced McTrax that doubles as a recording studio; it reacts to touch.<sup id="cite_ref-High_Tech_Capabilities_91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-High_Tech_Capabilities-91">[91]</a></sup> They can create their own beats with a <a href="/wiki/Synth" class="mw-redirect" title="Synth">synth</a> and tweak sounds with special effects.<sup id="cite_ref-High_Tech_Capabilities_91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-High_Tech_Capabilities-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_diet">Special diet</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McDonald%27s_kosher.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/McDonald%27s_kosher.JPG/220px-McDonald%27s_kosher.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="499"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 137px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/McDonald%27s_kosher.JPG/220px-McDonald%27s_kosher.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="137" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McDonald%27s_kosher.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A kosher <i>Express</i> McDonald's outlet in the <a href="/wiki/Malha_Mall" title="Malha Mall">Malha Mall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a></div></div></div>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b> with: Details of other McDonald's restaurants where special dietary requirements are catered for. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=McDonald%27s&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2015</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p>The first <a href="/wiki/Kosher" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosher">kosher</a> McDonald's was established in 1997 at the <a href="/wiki/Abasto_de_Buenos_Aires" title="Abasto de Buenos Aires">Abasto de Buenos Aires</a> mall in <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, Argentina. There are many kosher branches in Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Playgrounds">Playgrounds</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McDonald%27s_with_Prominent_Playland.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/McDonald%27s_with_Prominent_Playland.JPG/220px-McDonald%27s_with_Prominent_Playland.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/McDonald%27s_with_Prominent_Playland.JPG/220px-McDonald%27s_with_Prominent_Playland.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McDonald%27s_with_Prominent_Playland.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A McDonald's in <a href="/wiki/Panorama_City,_Los_Angeles" title="Panorama City, Los Angeles">Panorama City, Los Angeles</a>, California with a Playplace designed to promote a family-friendly image</div></div></div>
<p>McDonald's playgrounds are called McDonald's PlayPlace. Some McDonald's in suburban areas and certain cities feature large indoor or outdoor <a href="/wiki/Playground" title="Playground">playgrounds</a>. The first PlayPlace with the familiar crawl-tube design with ball pits and slides was introduced in 1987 in the US, with many more being constructed soon after.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="McDonald.27s_Next"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="McDonald's_Next">McDonald's Next</span></h4>
<p>McDonald's Next use open-concept design and offer "Create Your Taste" digital ordering. The concept store also offers free mobile device charging and table service after 6:00 pm. The first store opened in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn-20160111_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-20160111-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2006_redesign">2006 redesign</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_McDonald%27s_restaurant_in_Mount_Pleasant,_Iowa.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/New_McDonald%27s_restaurant_in_Mount_Pleasant%2C_Iowa.jpg/220px-New_McDonald%27s_restaurant_in_Mount_Pleasant%2C_Iowa.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="744"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 160px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/New_McDonald%27s_restaurant_in_Mount_Pleasant%2C_Iowa.jpg/220px-New_McDonald%27s_restaurant_in_Mount_Pleasant%2C_Iowa.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="160" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_McDonald%27s_restaurant_in_Mount_Pleasant,_Iowa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An American McDonald's in <a href="/wiki/Mount_Pleasant,_Iowa" title="Mount Pleasant, Iowa">Mount Pleasant, Iowa</a> in June 2008; this is an example of the "new" look of American McDonald's restaurants</div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, McDonald's introduced its "Forever Young" brand by redesigning all of its restaurants, the first major redesign since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p><p>The goal of the redesign is to be more like a coffee shop, similar to <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a>. The design includes wooden tables, faux-leather chairs, and muted colors; the red was muted to terracotta, the yellow was shifted to golden for a more "sunny" look, and olive and sage green were also added.
</p><p>To create a warmer look, the restaurants have less plastic and more brick and wood, with modern hanging lights to produce a softer glow. Many restaurants feature free <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> and flat-screen TVs. Other upgrades include double drive-thrus, flat roofs instead of the angled red roofs, and replacing fiber glass with wood. Instead of the familiar golden arches, the restaurants feature "semi-swooshes" (half of a golden arch), similar to the Nike <a href="/wiki/Swoosh" title="Swoosh">swoosh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Smoking_ban">Smoking ban</span></h3>
<p>McDonald's began banning smoking in 1994 when it restricted customers from smoking within its 1,400 wholly owned restaurants.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment_of_employees">Treatment of employees</span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Denton_House_LI_03_-_Order_kiosk.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Denton_House_LI_03_-_Order_kiosk.jpg/220px-Denton_House_LI_03_-_Order_kiosk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3864" data-file-height="5152"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 293px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Denton_House_LI_03_-_Order_kiosk.jpg/220px-Denton_House_LI_03_-_Order_kiosk.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="293" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Denton_House_LI_03_-_Order_kiosk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Interactive_kiosk#Restaurant_kiosk" title="Interactive kiosk">kiosk</a> for placing orders at the <a href="/wiki/Denton_House_(New_York)" class="mw-redirect" title="Denton House (New York)">Denton House</a> McDonald's in New Hyde Park, on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>, New York</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bauan,Batangaschurchjf9602_17.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bauan%2CBatangaschurchjf9602_17.JPG/220px-Bauan%2CBatangaschurchjf9602_17.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bauan%2CBatangaschurchjf9602_17.JPG/220px-Bauan%2CBatangaschurchjf9602_17.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bauan,Batangaschurchjf9602_17.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A McDonald's employee takes an order in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a></div></div></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Automation">Automation</span></h3>
<p>Since the late 1990s, McDonald's has attempted to replace employees with electronic kiosks which would perform actions such as taking orders and accepting money. In 1999, McDonald's first tested "E-Clerks" in suburban <a href="/wiki/Chicago,_Illinois" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago, Illinois">Chicago, Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming,_Michigan" title="Wyoming, Michigan">Wyoming, Michigan</a>, with the devices being able to "save money on live staffers" and attracting larger purchase amounts than average employees.<sup id="cite_ref-ECLERK_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECLERK-99">[99]</a></sup></p><p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> estimated that in the succeeding decades, there was a 92% probability of food preparation and serving to become automated in fast food establishments.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> By 2016, McDonald's "Create Your Taste" electronic kiosks were seen in some restaurants internationally where customers could custom order meals. As employees pushed for higher wages in the late-2010s, some believed that fast food companies such as McDonald's would use the devices to cut costs for employing individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2019, McDonald's purchased an AI-based start-up Apprente for replacing human servers with voice-based technology in its US drive-throughs.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Wages">Wages</span></h3>
<p>On August 5, 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> revealed that 90 percent of McDonald's UK workforce are on <a href="/wiki/Zero_hour_contract" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero hour contract">zero hour contracts</a>, making it possibly the largest such private sector employer in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In April 2017, due to employee strikes, they gave all employees the option of fixed contracts instead.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> A study released by Fast Food Forward conducted by Anzalone Liszt Grove Research showed that approximately 84 percent of all fast food employees working in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> in April 2013 had been paid less than their legal wages by their employers.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p><p>From 2007 to 2011, fast food workers in the U.S. drew an average of $7 billion of <a href="/wiki/Public_assistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Public assistance">public assistance</a> annually resulting from receiving low wages.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The McResource website advised employees to break their food into smaller pieces to feel fuller, seek refunds for unopened holiday purchases, sell possessions online for quick cash, and to "quit complaining" as "stress hormone levels rise by 15 percent after ten minutes of complaining."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In December 2013, McDonald's shut down the McResource website amidst negative publicity and criticism. McDonald's plans to continue an internal telephone help line through which its employees can obtain advice on work and life problems.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Liberal_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal (politics)">Liberal</a> <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> the <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Institute" title="Roosevelt Institute">Roosevelt Institute</a> accuses some McDonald's restaurants of actually paying less than the minimum wage to entry positions due to 'rampant' <a href="/wiki/Wage_theft" title="Wage theft">wage theft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, McDonald's pays part-time employees $5.50 an hour and is accused of paying less with arbitrary schedules adjustments and pay delays.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Korea_Observer_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Korea_Observer-110">[110]</a></sup> In late 2015, <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_(group)" title="Anonymous (group)">Anonymous</a> aggregated data collected by <a href="/wiki/Glassdoor" title="Glassdoor">Glassdoor</a> suggests that McDonald's in the United States pays entry-level employees between $7.25 an hour and $11 an hour, with an average of $8.69 an hour. Shift managers get paid an average of $10.34 an hour. Assistant managers get paid an average of $11.57 an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> McDonald's CEO, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Easterbrook" title="Steve Easterbrook">Steve Easterbrook</a>, earns an annual salary of $1,100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> His total compensation for 2017 was $21,761,052.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Strikes">Strikes</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fast_food_worker_strikes" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast food worker strikes">Fast food worker strikes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fast_food_workers_on_strike_for_higher_minimum_wage_and_better_benefits_(26398607316).jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Fast_food_workers_on_strike_for_higher_minimum_wage_and_better_benefits_%2826398607316%29.jpg/220px-Fast_food_workers_on_strike_for_higher_minimum_wage_and_better_benefits_%2826398607316%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="7360" data-file-height="4140"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Fast_food_workers_on_strike_for_higher_minimum_wage_and_better_benefits_%2826398607316%29.jpg/220px-Fast_food_workers_on_strike_for_higher_minimum_wage_and_better_benefits_%2826398607316%29.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fast_food_workers_on_strike_for_higher_minimum_wage_and_better_benefits_(26398607316).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fast food workers on strike outside of a McDonald's in <a href="/wiki/St._Paul,_Minnesota" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Paul, Minnesota">St. Paul, Minnesota</a></div></div></div>
<p>McDonald's workers have on occasions decided to <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike</a> over pay, with most of the employees on strike seeking to be paid $15.00.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> When interviewed about the strikes occurring, former McDonald's CEO Ed Rensi stated: "It's cheaper to buy a $35,000 robotic arm than it is to hire an employee who's inefficient making $15 an hour bagging french fries" with Rensi explaining that increasing employee wages could possibly take away from entry-level jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-BIceo_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIceo-115">[115]</a></sup> However, according to Easterbrook, increasing wages and benefits for workers saw a 6% increase in customer satisfaction when comparing 2015's first quarter data to the first quarter of 2016, with greater returns seen as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-BIceo_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIceo-115">[115]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2017, two British McDonald's stores agreed to a strike over <a href="/wiki/Zero-hours_contract" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero-hours contract">zero-hours contracts</a> for staff. Picket lines were formed around the two stores in <a href="/wiki/Crayford" title="Crayford">Crayford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>. The strike was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a> <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupation">Occupation</span></h3>
<p>Workers at the McDonald's franchise at Saint-Barthelemy, <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, occupied the restaurant, in protest against its planned closure. Employing 77 people the restaurant is the second-biggest private sector employer in Saint-Barthélémy, which has an unemployment rate of 30 per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Lawyers for Kamel Guemari, a shop steward at the franchise, claimed an attempt was made to kill him when a car drove at him in the restaurant car park.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_conditions">Working conditions</span></h3>
<p>In March 2015, McDonald's workers in 19 U.S. cities filed 28 health and safety complaints with the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> which allege that low staffing, lack of protective gear, poor training and pressure to work fast has resulted in injuries. The complaints allege that, because of a lack of <a href="/wiki/First_aid" title="First aid">first aid</a> supplies, workers were told by management to treat <a href="/wiki/Burn_injuries" class="mw-redirect" title="Burn injuries">burn injuries</a> with condiments such as <a href="/wiki/Mayonnaise" title="Mayonnaise">mayonnaise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mustard_(condiment)" title="Mustard (condiment)">mustard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The Fight for $15 labor organization aided the workers in filing the complaints.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare_standards">Animal welfare standards</span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<p>In 2015, McDonald's pledged to stop using eggs from <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cage</a> facilities by 2025. Since McDonald's purchases over 2 billion eggs per year or 4 percent of eggs produced in the United States, the switch is expected to have a major impact on the egg industry and is part of a general trend toward cage-free eggs driven by consumer concern over the harsh living conditions of hens.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wired2016_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired2016-124">[124]</a></sup> The aviary systems from which the new eggs will be sourced are troubled by much higher mortality rates, as well as introducing environmental and worker safety problems.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The high hen mortality rate, which is more than double that of battery cage systems, will require new research to mitigate. The facilities have higher ammonia levels due to faeces being kicked up into the air. Producers raised concerns about the production cost, which is expected to increase by 36 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p><p>McDonald's continues to source pork from facilities that use <a href="/wiki/Gestation_crates" class="mw-redirect" title="Gestation crates">gestation crates</a>, and in 2012 pledged to phase them out.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing_and_advertising">Marketing and advertising</span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_advertising" title="McDonald's advertising">McDonald's advertising</a></div>
<p>McDonald's has for decades maintained an extensive advertising campaign. In addition to the usual media (television, radio, and newspaper), the company makes significant use of billboards and signage, and also sponsors sporting events ranging from <a href="/wiki/Little_League_Baseball" title="Little League Baseball">Little League</a> to the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Television has played a central role in the company's advertising strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> To date, McDonald's has used 23 different slogans in United States advertising, as well as a few other slogans for select countries and regions.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Children.27s_advertising"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children's_advertising">Children's advertising</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ronald_McDonald" title="Ronald McDonald">Ronald McDonald</a> and <a href="/wiki/McDonaldland" title="McDonaldland">McDonaldland</a></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_exploration">Space exploration</span></h3>
<p>McDonald's and NASA explored an advertising agreement for a planned mission to the asteroid <a href="/wiki/449_Hamburga" title="449 Hamburga">449 Hamburga</a>; however, the spacecraft was eventually cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sponsorship_in_NASCAR">Sponsorship in NASCAR</span></h3>
<p>McDonald's entered the <a href="/wiki/NASCAR_Cup_Series" title="NASCAR Cup Series">NASCAR Cup series</a> in 1977, sponsoring <a href="/wiki/Richard_Childress" title="Richard Childress">Richard Childress</a> for one race. Between the years 1977 and 1986, McDonald's would only sponsor a handful of races in a season. In 1993, McDonald's would become the full-time sponsor for the No. 27 <a href="/wiki/Junior_Johnson_%26_Associates" title="Junior Johnson &amp; Associates">Junior Johnson &amp; Associates</a> <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>, driven by <a href="/wiki/Hut_Stricklin" title="Hut Stricklin">Hut Stricklin</a>. In 1994, Stricklin would be replaced in the car by <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Spencer" title="Jimmy Spencer">Jimmy Spencer</a>, who would go on and win twice that season. The following season McDonald's would move over to the No. 94 <a href="/wiki/Bill_Elliott_Racing" title="Bill Elliott Racing">Bill Elliott Racing</a> Ford, driven by team-owner <a href="/wiki/Bill_Elliott" title="Bill Elliott">Bill Elliott</a>. McDonald's would stay will Elliott until the 2001 season when they moved again, this time to the No. 96 <a href="/wiki/PPI_Motorsports" title="PPI Motorsports">PPI Motorsports</a> Ford, driven by rookie <a href="/wiki/Andy_Houston" title="Andy Houston">Andy Houston</a>. However, when the team failed to field a car for the entire season, McDonald's became absent from NASCAR until 2004, when it joined Evernham Motorsports, becoming a part-time sponsor for drivers Bill Elliott, <a href="/wiki/Kasey_Kahne" title="Kasey Kahne">Kasey Kahne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elliott_Sadler" title="Elliott Sadler">Elliott Sadler</a>, <a href="/wiki/A._J._Allmendinger" title="A. J. Allmendinger">A.J. Allmendinger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reed_Sorenson" title="Reed Sorenson">Reed Sorenson</a> all the way until 2010. During the 2010 season, McDonald's would enter its longest partnership with a team. Sponsoring select races with <a href="/wiki/Chip_Ganassi_Racing" title="Chip Ganassi Racing">Chip Gannasi Racing</a>'s No. 1 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet" title="Chevrolet">Chevrolet</a>, driven by <a href="/wiki/Jamie_McMurray" title="Jamie McMurray">Jamie McMurray</a>, McDonald's would go and sponsor McMurray until his retirement in the 2019 Daytona 500. In 2019, McDonald's would stay with Chip Ganassi Racing, switching to the No. 42 car, driven by <a href="/wiki/Kyle_Larson" title="Kyle Larson">Kyle Larson</a>. McDonald's would also sponsor <a href="/wiki/Richard_Petty_Motorsports" title="Richard Petty Motorsports">Richard Petty Motorsports</a> No. 43 Chevrolet, driven by <a href="/wiki/Darrell_Wallace_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Darrell Wallace Jr.">Darrell Wallace Jr.</a>, for a single race. As of November 2019, McDonald's still sponsors the No. 42 Chevrolet driven by Kyle Larson.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_awards_and_honors">Sports awards and honors</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:McDonald%27s_High_School_All-Americans" title="Category:McDonald's High School All-Americans">Category:McDonald's High School All-Americans</a></div>
<p>McDonald's is the title sponsor of the <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_All-American_Game" title="McDonald's All-American Game">McDonald's All-American Game</a>, all-star basketball games played each year for American and Canadian boys' and girls' <a href="/wiki/High_school_basketball" class="mw-redirect" title="High school basketball">high school basketball</a> graduates.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ronald_McDonald_House_Charities" title="Ronald McDonald House Charities">Ronald McDonald House Charities</a></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="McHappy_Day">McHappy Day</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Feed_the_clown.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/72/Feed_the_clown.JPG/220px-Feed_the_clown.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2048"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 147px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/72/Feed_the_clown.JPG/220px-Feed_the_clown.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="147" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feed_the_clown.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Ronald McDonald House collection box in <a href="/wiki/Framingham,_Massachusetts" title="Framingham, Massachusetts">Framingham, Massachusetts</a></div></div></div>
<p>McHappy Day is an annual event at McDonald's, during which a percentage of the day's sales go to <a href="/wiki/Charitable_organization" title="Charitable organization">charity</a>. It is the signature fundraising event for <a href="/wiki/Ronald_McDonald_House_Charities" title="Ronald McDonald House Charities">Ronald McDonald House Charities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p><p>In 2007, it was celebrated in 17 countries: Argentina, Australia, Austria, Brazil, Canada, England, Finland, France, Guatemala, Hungary, Ireland, New Zealand, Norway, Sweden, Switzerland, the United States, and Uruguay.
</p><p>According to the Australian McHappy Day website, McHappy Day raised $20.4 million in 2009. The goal for 2010 was $20.8 million.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="McDonald.27s_Monopoly_donation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="McDonald's_Monopoly_donation">McDonald's Monopoly donation</span></h3>
<p>In 1995, <a href="/wiki/St._Jude_Children%27s_Research_Hospital" title="St. Jude Children's Research Hospital">St. Jude Children's Research Hospital</a> received an anonymous letter postmarked in Dallas, Texas, containing a $1 million winning <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_Monopoly" title="McDonald's Monopoly">McDonald's Monopoly</a> game piece. McDonald's officials came to the hospital, accompanied by a representative from the accounting firm Arthur Andersen, who examined the card under a jeweler's eyepiece, handled it with plastic gloves, and verified it as a winner.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Although game rules prohibited the transfer of prizes, McDonald's waived the rule and made the annual $50,000 annuity payments for the full 20-year period through 2014, even after learning that the piece was sent by an individual involved in an embezzlement scheme <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_Monopoly#Fraud" title="McDonald's Monopoly">intended to defraud McDonald's</a>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="McRefugee">McRefugee</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/McRefugee" title="McRefugee">McRefugee</a></div>
<p>McRefugees are poor people in Hong Kong, Japan, and China who use McDonald's 24-hour restaurants as a temporary hostel. One in five of Hong Kong's population lives below the poverty line. The rise of McRefugees was first documented by photographer Suraj Katra in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Centerfold_Stripper_Midgets_Chickens_39_2010_Shankbone.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Centerfold_Stripper_Midgets_Chickens_39_2010_Shankbone.jpg/220px-Centerfold_Stripper_Midgets_Chickens_39_2010_Shankbone.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2127" data-file-height="2659"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 275px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Centerfold_Stripper_Midgets_Chickens_39_2010_Shankbone.jpg/220px-Centerfold_Stripper_Midgets_Chickens_39_2010_Shankbone.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="275" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Centerfold_Stripper_Midgets_Chickens_39_2010_Shankbone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/People_for_the_Ethical_Treatment_of_Animals" title="People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals">PETA</a> activist dressed as a chicken confronts the manager of the <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">Times Square</a> McDonald's over the company's animal welfare standards.</div></div></div>
<p>In the late 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Phil_Sokolof" title="Phil Sokolof">Phil Sokolof</a>, a millionaire businessman who had suffered a heart attack at the age of 43, took out full-page newspaper ads in New York, Chicago, and other large cities accusing McDonald's menu of being a threat to American health, and asking them to stop using <a href="/wiki/Beef_tallow" class="mw-redirect" title="Beef tallow">beef tallow</a> to cook their french fries.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p><p>In 1990, activists from a small group known as <a href="/wiki/London_Greenpeace" title="London Greenpeace">London Greenpeace</a> (no connection to the international group <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>) distributed leaflets entitled <i>What's wrong with McDonald's?</i>, criticizing its environmental, health, and labor record. The corporation wrote to the group demanding they desist and apologize, and, when two of the activists refused to back down, sued them for <a href="/wiki/Defamation" title="Defamation">libel</a> leading to the "<a href="/wiki/McLibel_case" title="McLibel case">McLibel case</a>", one of the longest cases in British civil law. A documentary <a href="/wiki/McLibel_(film)" title="McLibel (film)">film of the McLibel Trial</a> has been shown in several countries.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p><p>In 2001, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Schlosser" title="Eric Schlosser">Eric Schlosser</a>'s book <i><a href="/wiki/Fast_Food_Nation" title="Fast Food Nation">Fast Food Nation</a></i> included criticism of the business practices of McDonald's. Among the critiques were allegations that McDonald's (along with other companies within the fast food industry) uses its political influence to increase its profits at the expense of people's health and the social conditions of its workers. The book also brought into question McDonald's advertisement techniques in which it targets children. While the book did mention other fast-food chains, it focused primarily on McDonald's.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>In 2002, vegetarian groups, largely <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a>, successfully sued McDonald's for misrepresenting its French fries as vegetarian, when they contained beef <a href="/wiki/Broth" title="Broth">broth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p><p>Though the company objected, the term "McJob" was added to <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster%27s_Collegiate_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Merriam-Webster's Collegiate Dictionary">Merriam-Webster's Collegiate Dictionary</a></i> in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-mwcd_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwcd-139">[139]</a></sup> The term was defined as "a low-paying job that requires little skill and provides little opportunity for advancement".<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Morgan_Spurlock" title="Morgan Spurlock">Morgan Spurlock</a>'s 2004 <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Super_Size_Me" title="Super Size Me">Super Size Me</a></i> claimed that McDonald's food was contributing to the increase of <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> in society and that the company was failing to provide nutritional information about its food for its customers. Six weeks after the film premiered, McDonald's announced that it was eliminating the super size option, and was creating the adult <a href="/wiki/Happy_Meal" title="Happy Meal">Happy Meal</a>. There was a documentary called <i><a href="/wiki/Fat_Head" title="Fat Head">Fat Head</a></i>, which pointed out inconsistencies in <i>Super Size Me</i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mcgameshot.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Mcgameshot.png/220px-Mcgameshot.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="688" data-file-height="381"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 122px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Mcgameshot.png/220px-Mcgameshot.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="122" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mcgameshot.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Screenshot from <i><a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_Video_Game" title="McDonald's Video Game">McDonald's Video Game</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>In 2006, an unsanctioned <i><a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_Video_Game" title="McDonald's Video Game">McDonald's Video Game</a></i> by Italian group <a href="/wiki/Molleindustria" title="Molleindustria">Molleindustria</a> was released online. It is parody of the business practices of the corporate giant, taking the guise of a tycoon style business simulation game. In the game, the player plays the role of a McDonald's CEO, choosing whether or not to use controversial practices like genetically altered cow feed, plowing over rainforests, and corrupting public officials. McDonald's issued a statement distancing itself from the game.<sup id="cite_ref-bluestein_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bluestein-141">[141]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2014, it was reported that McDonald's was accused of having used a series of tax maneuvers to avoid taxes in France. The company confirmed that tax authorities had visited McDonald's French headquarters in Paris but insisted that it had not done anything wrong, saying, "McDonald's firmly denies the accusation made by L'Express according to which McDonald's supposedly hid part of its revenue from taxes in France."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Company_responses_to_criticism">Company responses to criticism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:McDonald%27s_discreet_shopfront_at_historic_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/McDonald%27s_discreet_shopfront_at_historic_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg/220px-McDonald%27s_discreet_shopfront_at_historic_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="317" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1636" data-file-height="2358"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 317px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/McDonald%27s_discreet_shopfront_at_historic_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg/220px-McDonald%27s_discreet_shopfront_at_historic_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="317" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:McDonald%27s_discreet_shopfront_at_historic_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Discreet shopfront in historic <a href="/wiki/Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Stratford-upon-Avon">Stratford-upon-Avon</a></div></div></div>
<p>In response to public pressure, McDonald's has sought to include more healthy choices in its menu and has introduced a new slogan to its recruitment posters: "Not bad for a <a href="/wiki/McJob" title="McJob">McJob</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-mcjob_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcjob-143">[143]</a></sup> The word McJob, first attested in the mid-1980s<sup id="cite_ref-mwcd_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mwcd-139">[139]</a></sup> and later popularized by Canadian novelist <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Coupland" title="Douglas Coupland">Douglas Coupland</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Generation_X:_Tales_for_an_Accelerated_Culture" title="Generation X: Tales for an Accelerated Culture">Generation X: Tales for an Accelerated Culture</a></i>, has become a <a href="/wiki/Buzzword" title="Buzzword">buzzword</a> for low-paid, unskilled work with few prospects or benefits and little security. McDonald's disputes this definition of McJob. In 2007, the company launched an advertising campaign with the slogan "Would you like a career with that?" on <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Irish</a> television, asserting that its jobs have good prospects.
</p><p>In an effort to respond to growing consumer awareness of food provenance, the fast-food chain changed its <a href="/wiki/Distributor_(business)" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributor (business)">supplier</a> of both <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> beans and <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>. UK chief executive Steve Easterbrook said: "British consumers are increasingly interested in the quality, sourcing, and <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">ethics</a> of the food and drink they buy".<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In a bid to tap into the <a href="/wiki/Ethical_consumerism" title="Ethical consumerism">ethical consumer market</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> McDonald's switched to using coffee beans taken from stocks that are certified by the <a href="/wiki/Rainforest_Alliance" title="Rainforest Alliance">Rainforest Alliance</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest_conservation" title="Tropical rainforest conservation">conservation group</a>. Additionally, in response to pressure, McDonald's UK started using <a href="/wiki/Organic_milk" title="Organic milk">organic milk</a> supplies for its bottled milk and hot drinks, although it still uses conventional milk in its milkshakes, and in all of its dairy products in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-TruthOMeter,_2012_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TruthOMeter,_2012-146">[146]</a></sup> According to a report published by <i><a href="/wiki/Farmers_Weekly" title="Farmers Weekly">Farmers Weekly</a></i> in 2007, the quantity of milk used by McDonald's could have accounted for as much as 5 percent of the UK's organic milk output.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p><p>McDonald's announced on May 22, 2008, that, in the United States and Canada, it would switch to using cooking oil that contains no <a href="/wiki/Trans_fat" title="Trans fat">trans fats</a> for its <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">french fries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canola" class="mw-redirect" title="Canola">canola</a>-based oil with corn and soy oils, for its baked items, pies and cookies, by year's end.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p><p>With regard to acquiring chickens from suppliers who use <a href="/wiki/Controlled_atmosphere_stunning" class="mw-redirect" title="Controlled atmosphere stunning">CAK/CAS methods of slaughter</a>, McDonald's says that it needs to see more research "to help determine whether any CAS system in current use is optimal from an animal welfare perspective."<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_record">Environmental record</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stagnant_water_McDonalds_cup.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Stagnant_water_McDonalds_cup.jpg/220px-Stagnant_water_McDonalds_cup.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="256" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="1117"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 256px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Stagnant_water_McDonalds_cup.jpg/220px-Stagnant_water_McDonalds_cup.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="256" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stagnant_water_McDonalds_cup.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stagnant water in a discarded McDonald's plastic cup.</div></div></div>
<p>Since McDonald's began receiving criticism for its environmental practices in the 1970s, it has significantly reduced its use of materials.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> For instance, an "average meal" in the 1970s—a Big Mac, fries, and a drink—required 46 grams (1.6 oz) of packaging; today, it requires 25 grams (0.88 oz), a 46 percent reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> In addition, McDonald's eliminated the need for intermediate containers for <a href="/wiki/Cola" title="Cola">cola</a> by using a delivery system that pumps syrup directly from the delivery truck into storage containers, saving two million pounds (910 tonnes) of packaging annually.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Weight reductions in packaging and products, as well as increased usage of bulk packaging, ultimately decreased packaging by twenty-four million pounds (11,000 tonnes) annually.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> McDonald's efforts to reduce solid waste by using less packaging and by promoting the use of recycled materials were recognized by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Environmental_Protection_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Environmental Protection Agency">U.S. Environmental Protection Agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p><p>In 1990, McDonald's worked with the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Defense_Fund" title="Environmental Defense Fund">Environmental Defense Fund</a> to stop using "<a href="/wiki/Clamshell_(container)" title="Clamshell (container)">clam shell</a>"-shaped <a href="/wiki/Foam_food_container" title="Foam food container">styrofoam food containers</a> to store its food products.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2008, McDonald's announced that 11 of its restaurants in <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a>, England, were engaged in a biomass trial program that cut its waste and carbon footprint by half in the area. In this trial, waste from the restaurants was collected by <a href="/wiki/Veolia_Environmental_Services" title="Veolia Environmental Services">Veolia Environmental Services</a> and used to produce energy at a power plant. McDonald's announced plans to expand this project, although the lack of biomass power plants in the United States would prevent adoption of this plan as a national standard there anytime soon.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In addition, in Europe, McDonald's has been recycling vegetable grease by converting it to fuel for its diesel trucks.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p><p>In an effort to reduce energy usage by 25 percent in its restaurants, McDonald's opened a prototype restaurant in Chicago in 2009, intending to use the model in its other restaurants throughout the world. Building on past efforts, specifically a restaurant it opened in Sweden in 2000 that was the first to incorporate green ideas, McDonald's designed the Chicago site to save energy by managing storm water, using skylights for more natural lighting, and installing partitions and tabletops made from recycled goods, among other measures.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p><p>In 2012, McDonald's announced they would trial replacing styrofoam coffee cups with an alternative material.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p><p>In 2019, McDonalds switched from <a href="/wiki/Plastic_straw" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastic straw">plastic straws</a> to paper ones in Ireland and the United Kingdom in an effort to reduce plastic pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-axed_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axed-161">[161]</a></sup> However, a subsequent investigation by <a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(United_Kingdom)" title="The Sun (United Kingdom)"><i>The Sun</i></a> found that the new paper straws were not recyclable.<sup id="cite_ref-axed_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-axed-161">[161]</a></sup> Disabled activists criticized the shift away from plastic straws as <a href="/wiki/Ableism" title="Ableism">ableist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p><p>McDonald's uses a corn-based <a href="/wiki/Bioplastic" title="Bioplastic">bioplastic</a> to produce containers for some products. The environmental benefits of this technology are controversial, with critics noting that biodegradation is slow and produces greenhouse gases, and that contamination of traditional <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic waste</a> streams with <a href="/wiki/Bioplastics" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioplastics">bioplastics</a> can complicate recycling efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p><p>Studies of <a href="/wiki/Litter" title="Litter">litter</a> have found that McDonald's is one of the most littered brands worldwide.  In 2012, a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Keep_Australia_Beautiful&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Keep Australia Beautiful (page does not exist)">Keep Australia Beautiful</a> study found that McDonald's was the most littered brand in <a href="/wiki/Queensland,_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Queensland, Australia">Queensland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> In 2009, <a href="/wiki/Keep_Britain_Tidy" title="Keep Britain Tidy">Keep Britain Tidy</a> likewise found McDonald's to be the leading producer of fast-food litter on British streets, accounting for 29% of the total. <sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> An early protest against this practice was "Operation Send-It-Back", launched by <a href="/wiki/London_Greenpeace" title="London Greenpeace">London Greenpeace</a> in 1994 in response to the company's targeting of activists in the <a href="/wiki/McLibel_Trial" class="mw-redirect" title="McLibel Trial">McLibel Trial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-deegan_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deegan-167">[167]</a></sup> Participants in Operation Send-It-Back returned 30 sacks of McDonald's litter to the company.<sup id="cite_ref-deegan_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deegan-167">[167]</a></sup>  In the 2010s, similar individual protests took in New Zealand<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> and England.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_cases">Legal cases</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/McDonald%27s_legal_cases" title="McDonald's legal cases">McDonald's legal cases</a></div>
<p>McDonald's has been involved in a number of <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuits</a> and other legal cases, most of which involved <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> disputes. The company has threatened many food businesses with legal action unless it drops the Mc or Mac from trading names.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span></h4>
<p>In April 2017, Irish fast-food chain <a href="/wiki/Supermac%27s" title="Supermac's">Supermac's</a> submitted a request to the European Union Property Office to cancel McDonald's owned trademarks within the European Union, claiming that McDonald's engaged in "trademark bullying; registering brand names... which are simply stored away in a war chest to use against future competitors", after the trademarks had prevented Supermac's from expanding out of Ireland. The EUIPO ruled in Supermac's favour, finding that McDonald's "has not proven genuine use" of many trademarks, cancelling McDonald's owned trademarks such as 'Big Mac' and certain 'Mc' related trademarks within the European Union.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Burger_King" title="Burger King">Burger King</a> responded by trolling McDonald's by giving their sandwiches names like "Like a Big Mac But Juicier", "Like a Big Mac, But Actually Big" and "Big Mac-ish But Flame-Grilled of Course".<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span></h4>
<p>On September 8, 2009, McDonald's Malaysian operations lost a lawsuit to prevent another restaurant calling itself McCurry. McDonald's lost in an appeal to Malaysia's highest court, the Federal Court.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span></h4>
<p>In April 2007, in <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>, Western Australia, McDonald's pleaded guilty to five charges relating to the employment of children under 15 in one of its outlets and was fined A$8,000.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span></h4>
<p>The longest-running legal action of all time in the UK was the <a href="/wiki/McLibel_case" title="McLibel case">McLibel case</a> against two defendants who criticized a number of aspects of the company. The trial lasted 10 years and called 130 witnesses. The <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> deemed that the unequal resources of the litigants breached the defendants  rights to freedom of speech and biased the trial. The result was widely seen as a "PR disaster" for McDonald's.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<p>A famous legal case in the U.S. involving McDonald's was the 1994 decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Liebeck_v._McDonald%27s_Restaurants" title="Liebeck v. McDonald's Restaurants">Liebeck v. McDonald's Restaurants</a></i>, where <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque, New Mexico</a> resident Stella Liebeck was initially awarded $2.86 million (equivalent to $4.49 million in 2018)<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-USGDP_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-USGDP-177">[177]</a></sup> after 81-year-old Liebeck suffered third-degree burns after spilling a scalding cup of McDonald's coffee on herself. The award was later reduced to $640,000 (equivalent to $1 million in 2018).<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> In 2019, a McDonald's employee, Jenna Ries, sued the restaurant chain over allowing <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a> in the work place and described the <a href="/wiki/Workplace" title="Workplace">working environment</a> as "toxic".<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(10)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2><section class="mf-section-10 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-10">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arcos_Dorados_Holdings" title="Arcos Dorados Holdings">Arcos Dorados Holdings</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Founder_(film)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Founder (film)">The Founder</a></i> — a 2016 a biopic of Ray Kroc, detailing the creation and controversial business history of McDonald's</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_hamburger_restaurants" title="List of hamburger restaurants">List of hamburger restaurants</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_with_McDonald%27s_restaurants" title="List of countries with McDonald's restaurants">List of countries with McDonald's restaurants</a></li>
